### Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2005-02-13T20:47:34+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com>`

```
A complete "idiot" (I won't tell you what others call him) has posted a 
challenge in the PL/I newsgroup - based on his post in the Fortran newsgroup - 
in response to a C++ challenge <G>.

I responded in the PL/I newsgroup, that I wouldn't bother with this until it was 
asked by a valid COBOL programmer in the the COBOL newsgroup, but after looking 
at it, I thought it *MIGHT* be fun for some of us to actually come up with a 
COBOL soltuion.  (Depending upon table size restrictions, I think that an 
UNSTRING and SEARCH solution should be medium easy to do).

Anyone interested in trying? (Besides requested timing, also include your 
compiler and OS used in the solution)

P.S. <joke> Extra credit for using ANSI/ISO ('85, '89, or '02) conforming 
syntax - except for processing of Line Sequential input/output.

> -------------  below posted in comp.lang.fortran ----------
> Want to give this a shot?
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

#### ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-13T16:48:36-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108342116.221843.109250@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com>`

```
>> Pls post  your time and PC speed..

If the 'winner' is the fastest then the uniqueness mechanism chosen is
likely to be more influential than the language it is coded in.

 > total words    = 789781
> unique words =  12691

This indicates that on average there will be 64 or so lookups for each
word.  The program will only execute the 'new word found' code 12691
times wheras it will do the 'is this unique' 789781 times.

Short words are more likely to have a very high frequency.

For example the program may be coded as a simple array or as a pointer
list and the time will be long because of the search time.

It could be done faster using a binary or n-way tree in an array or a
dynamic structure.

Or it could be done using a hash table (with overflow).

I would guess that a 27-way tree could be fastest.  An array of many
thousand 'usage index occurs 27' items. The 27th represents end-word.
This would probably need to be dynamic.

This should mean that a word can be determined as alreay seen in n
double index hits where word size is n.

The absolute fastest would be using a hash using all letters that will
generate a unique hash.  This would require a gigabyte of RAM if each
word set a bit and only 7 letter words were considered.

Perhaps a combination mechanism.  For words of 4 letters or less use a
hash into a half megabyte array of bytes.  For longer words use the
27-way b-tree calculating each 2nd index from the letter.

As they say, a QuickSort in interpreted BASIC is likely to outperform a
hand coded assembler bubble sort.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-14T10:16:11-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108404971.639586.104170@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9Qs5Ar29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <1108342116.221843.109250@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <9Qs5Ar29flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
>> I would guess that a 27-way tree could be fastest.  An array of many
>> thousand 'usage index occurs 27' items. The 27th represents
end-word.

> I do not really understand what you mean.

It is necessary to identify quickly whether a word has already been
added to the structure. By converting a word into a series of numeric
values a->1, b->2 etc, endword->27 we can use these values as indexes:

       01  word-check.
             03  Word-letter         PIC 99 COMP-5 OCCURS 30.

       01  WA-Limit                  PIC 9(8) COMP-5 VALUE 1.
       01  WA-Index                 PIC 9(8) COMP-5.
       01  Word-array.
             03  WA-item            OCCURS 100000.
               05 WA-letter          PIC 9(8) COMP-5 OCCURS 27.

MOVE ALL LOW-VALUES   TO Word-array

using word 'adam' -> values in word-letter(n)  of  1, 4, 1, 13, 27, 0,
...

approximately and untested:

|    MOVE 1    TO WA-Index
|    PERFORM varying n from 1 by 1 until word-letter(n) = ZERO
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
|    end-perform

Note that 'add-word' will be called several times for each actual new
word so it needs to only count 1 and then 'switch off' until the next
word is tried.

The 'end word' value of 27 is required so that the code can distinguish
between 'adam' and 'adams'.
```

```
Richard wrote:
> 
> >> Pls post  your time and PC speed..
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
<snip>

The 'easiest' way to do the counting is to do it as a SORT: in the input
procedure extract all the words and release them; in the output
procedure just return them and do the counts.  This is (or was, anyway)
the standard method for concordances and word counts.  I'd be interested
to hear of comparative results for sorting vs. in-memory tables.

I find it mildly alarming that the text of the Bible is posted on
"patriot.net": the implication is disturbing.

PL
```

```
For those interested in a NEW version of my program that:

A) runs MUCH faster (under 10 seconds on my machine)

B) Uses a COBOL internal sort, but

C) creates no (large) output files,

D) Is still ANSI '85/'89 conforming (other than LINE sequential for input)

See:

 http://home.comcast.net/~wmklein/DOX/WORDCNT2.txt


If someone else wants to "tune" it further by converting my INSPECT and UNSTRING 
statements into "PERFORM loops" - I would (personally) be interested in what 
difference that would make (with different compilers and O/S)
```

```
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL

WMK> If someone else wants to "tune" it further by converting my INSPECT
WMK> and UNSTRING statements into "PERFORM loops" - I would
WMK> (personally) be interested in what difference that would make
WMK> (with different compilers and O/S)

   well, I do not really want to write a program myself (actually I  
should better work on my customer project instead of reading  
Netnews...), but your "parse-inp" section would look different ...


    Rather something like this:

--------- schnipp -----------------------------------------

  READ input-file
  PERFORM UNTIL NOT has-reached-EOF IN input-file-status
    MOVE = TO Pointer-Char-in-Line
    *> have a variable containing the line length ...
    PERFORM UNTIL Pointer-Char-in-Line >= length-of-Line
      *> skipping over eventual leading spaces
      INSPECT text-line
          TALLYING Pointer-Char-in-Line FOR LEADING SPACES
      ADD 1 TO Pointer-Char-in-Line
      MOVE ZERO TO word-length
      PERFORM WITH TEST AFTER
          UNTIL delimiter-found NOT = "'"
             OR word-length = 0
        UNSTRING text-line
            DELIMITED BY ALL SPACES
                         OR "." OR "," OR ":" OR ";" OR "-" OR "'"
                         OR "?" OR "!" OR "(" OR ")" OR '"'
            INTO word-found(word-length - 1:)
              DELIMITER IN delimiter-found
              COUNT IN word-length
            WITH POINTER Pointer-Char-in-Line
            TALLYING IN word-count-per-line
      END-PERFORM  *> skipping over apostrophs
      IF  word-length = 0
        OR word-found NUMERIC
      THEN
        SUBTRACT 1 FROM word-count-per-line
        *> is the above line really necessary? Otherwise: CONTINUE
      ELSE
        PERFORM process-word-found
      END-IF
    END-PERFORM  *> finding individual words in one line
    READ input-file
  END-PERFORM  *> processing lines read and fetch the next line


------------------ schnapp --------------------------------

  I have just typed this in the newsreader, and have neither checked  
the syntax nor some questions like if I have to increment the char- 
pointer after each UNSTRING

  The two main differentiating ideas compared to yours is that this  
code looks at each character of the input line once, and that there is  
not a table of words per line, but the words are being extracted and  
processed word by word.



Yours,
```

```
        MOVE 0 TO Pointer-Char-in-Line

   or better

        MOVE ZERO TO Pointer-Char-in-Line


LW>     *> have a variable containing the line length ...
LW>     PERFORM UNTIL Pointer-Char-in-Line >= length-of-Line

an english keyboard...

   And in the loop to put together words from parts to the left and to  
the right of an apostroph:


LW>         UNSTRING text-line
LW>             DELIMITED BY ALL SPACES
LW>                          OR "." OR "," OR ":" OR ";" OR "-" OR "'"
LW>                          OR "?" OR "!" OR "(" OR ")" OR '"'
LW>             INTO word-found(word-length - 1:)

   there should be a + (plus) sign in the reference modification, not  
a minus (-) sign. Not so much a typing error, but an eror to forget  
what has been thought out ... after writing:

LW>       MOVE ZERO TO word-length

   ts, ts, ts
```

```
William M. Klein wrote:
> For those interested in a NEW version of my program that:
> 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> difference that would make (with different compilers and O/S)

I played with this and played with this - an array with "SEARCH" is 
*not* the best COBOL way to solve this challenge.  :)

Using Perform loops with character-by-character moves using indexed 
tables, it took about 30 minutes.  Changed the actual moves to an 
UNSTRING, and it took 25 minutes.  Of course, that doesn't account for 
additional load on the machine, but we've got teeny weeny machines for 
our development platforms...
```

```
Just out of curiosity, did you try MY "version 2" program.  If it was what took 
20 minutes, that would really surprise me (on any O/S - with any compiler).

Obviously, change the Select/Assign clause (and get rid of the initial @OPTIONS 
statement) but other than that, I *think* my code should work with any '85/'89 
Standard compiler. (It could be changed to work with an '85 Standard compiler, 
but I used a couple of intrinsic functions for "easy" stuff like CURRENT-DATE 
and UPPER-CASE - the former could be done with an ACCEPT and the later with an 
INSPECT CONVERTING)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-20T04:10:23-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<helg11dbj17d2h5ffpcro7eskb0v7bskqj@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<4214D3BE.2B45AE71@mb.sympatico.ca> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <1108693202.508508.319200@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <r2pa11thn9j6kg3ekhs62ha6r2eqe04qb9@4ax.com> <1108701600.337896.265630@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <0vad11hnfse2tv2v1hglfnjhs55qe6qgub@4ax.com> <1108787503.329008.64020@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <21of115rt5b36ceq42su2mpfuqp37n9hgf@4ax.com> <1108877540.850547.216850@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 19 Feb 2005 21:32:20 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>>> So now you increase the code bloat by having a thread lock check at
>>> each node. You will spending more time in code execution than 3 CPUs
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>discussion.

My code did lock tree nodes in common memory before writing to them. I
did it the offical way, then showed how to do it faster.  Comments
described the logic in some detail. 

-- begin quotation --
* When building a tree with parallel processing, we must lock forward
* pointers before updating them to prevent 'collisions' -- two threads
* updating the same pointer at the same time. This demo shows two
* techniques for doing that. Case 13 uses mutex (mutual exclusion),
the
* lowest level, fastest and universally available of the
synchronization
* tools in the threads library. The others, not used here, are
monitor,
* semaphore and event, which are functionally similar to mutex.
*
* A mutex is a 'token' that can have only one owner. This demo uses
* 50,000 mutexes, one per node. Before updating a node, it asks for
* exclusive ownership of the node's mutex. If ownership cannot be
* obtained, it asks the thread library to put the thread to sleep
until
* the mutex is available. Then it updates the node and releases the
* mutex.
*
* Case 14 uses a novel method of self-synchronization. Rather than
* calling a library function, it avoids collisions by simply
* manipulating flags in shared memory. It uses an array of busy
* indicators, one per thread, puts its quarter on the edge of the pool
* table, then counts the number of quarters up there. If the result is
* 1, it MAY be the exclusive owner. However, while it was counting
* ..5..6..7.. another thread may have put a quarter in slot 1.
Wouldn't
* thread 1 count 2 quarters and give up for awhile? Not necessarily.
* Thread 1 may be suspended during its counting. By the time it wakes
* up, this thread's quarter has been removed. To handle that case, the
* program checks whether the pointer is still zero after counting one
* stakeholder. It will see the pointer deposited by this thread and
* thereby avoid a collision.
*
* The speed difference between the two methods is dramatic. Case 13,
* mutex, took 5.9 seconds -- 3.3 times slower than single-threading.
* Case 14 took .6 seconds, 3 times faster than single-threading, on a
* machine with 4 CPUs. The mutex function overhead defeats
* multitasking's promise of higher speed due to parallel processing.
* However, self-synchronization delivers the expected performance.


>> Detecting duplicates doesn't cause contention.
>
>Yes it will.  It will cause contention when two threads try to update
>the same node to, for example, add the same word.

Duplicates are detected by by reading the tree, not by writing to it.
Reading requires no locks. The case of two threads trying to insert
the same new word at the same time is a rare exception. When you
assert duplicate detection requires locks, you're misleading the
reader.

>> Counting word frequency would cause it,
>
>That, too will cause contention.  Code will have to check for it and
>apply locks.

That's what I said. Detecting a duplicate requires no locks, but
adding 1 to a duplicate counter on each node requires locking the
node.

>> as it did in your Python solution.
>
>Explain please.  Or did you just completely not understand what the
>exception handling was doing.

When you added duplicate counting, your execution time went up 30%.

>> The resulting slowdown is relatively small.
>
>So you do agree that there will be 'slowdown' at least.

A gross count of duplicates doesn't slow the process. It can be done
in the single-threaded parser. Counting occurrences per word requires
a lockable counter on each node (word). I don't think the original
problem spec called for that. It appears to have been added later.
Your first Python solution didn't have it; your second did.
```

```
> Duplicates are detected by by reading the tree, not by writing to it.
> Reading requires no locks.

An if one thread is checking to see if a word exists while another
thread is about to insert it but hasn't got CPU time yet then it will
find it not there and will try to insert.  You will get duplicate
insertions.
```

```
On Tue, 22 Feb 2005 12:58:05 -0800, "Chuck Stevens"
<charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:i5pf11tra6gjjono142n8auhc2g966143k@4ax.com...

>One man's "error" is another man's "carefully-considered design decision."

Yeahbut, the distinction between public and private is not
situational.

Common Sense  
Surprising new research shows that crowds are often smarter than
individuals  
By Michael Shermer  
 
 There is now overwhelming evidence, artfully accumulated and
articulated by New Yorker columnist James Surowiecki in his
enthralling 2004 book, The Wisdom of Crowds (Doubleday), that "the
many are smarter than the few." In one experiment, participants were
asked to estimate the number of jelly beans in a jar. The group
average was 871, only 2.5 percent off the actual figure of 850. Only
one of the 56 subjects was closer. The reason is that in a group,
individual errors on either side of the true figure cancel each other
out. 

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
For a group to be smart, it should be autonomous, decentralized and
diverse. 
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

A similar result was discovered in an example so counterintuitive that
it startles. When the U.S. submarine Scorpion disappeared in May 1968,
a naval scientist named John Craven assembled a diverse group of
submarine experts, mathematicians and salvage divers. Instead of
putting them in a room to consult one another, he had each of them
give a best guesstimate--based on the sub's last known speed and
position (and nothing else)--of the cause of its demise and its rate
and steepness of descent, among other variables. Craven then computed
a group average employing Bayes's theorem, a statistical method
wherein a probability is assigned to each component of a problem. The
Scorpion's location on the ocean floor was only 220 yards from the
averaged prediction. 

 
Stranger still was the stock market's reaction on January 28, 1986,
the day the space shuttle Challenger exploded. Of the four major
shuttle contractors--Lockheed, Rockwell International, Martin Marietta
and Morton Thiokol--the last (the builder of the defective
solid-rocket booster) was hit hardest, with a 12 percent loss,
compared with only 3 percent for the others. A detailed study of the
market (a sizable crowd, indeed!) by economists Michael T. Maloney of
Clemson University and J. Harold Mulherin of Claremont McKenna College
could find no evidence of insider trading or media focus on the rocket
booster or on Morton Thiokol. Given four possibilities, the masses
voted correctly.  

http://www.sciam.com/article.cfm?chanID=sa006&colID=13&articleID=00049F3E-91E1-119B-8EA483414B7FFE9F
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:
>  There is now overwhelming evidence, artfully accumulated and
> articulated by New Yorker columnist James Surowiecki in his
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Both those examples point to the exact opposite of your premise. In both 
cases, a large number of participants acted as individuals, NOT as a 
group. The groups quoted were not a "smart groups". The results were 
obtained by isolating individuals, ensuring the individuals did NOT act 
as a group, and extracting a result through statistics.

If your premise had been "For a group to be smart, it cannot act as a 
group, it must not be a group, and the members must be chosen based on 
their isolation from each other", then your examples may have been germaine.

Frankly, I do not really see anything unusual or new in the fact that 
people investing a lot in the defence industry know more than average 
about the defence industry, but maybe that's just me. To think it 
startling new knowledge and a counter-intuitive revelation of human 
nature seems kinda weird.

BTW, do you have an URL to the "contest" solution you posted?  What were 
your benchmarks?  I could not find them in here or on Google.

Donald
```

- **From:** l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms)
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T08:04:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9R7h1kFPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <1108693202.508508.319200@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <r2pa11thn9j6kg3ekhs62ha6r2eqe04qb9@4ax.com>`

```
.    On  18.02.05
  wrote  spamblocker-robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
     on  /COMP/LANG/COBOL
     in  r2pa11thn9j6kg3ekhs62ha6r2eqe04qb9@4ax.com
  about  Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)


>> Please show us, Robert, show us how you will parallel process by
>> starting 8 million threads, one for each word.

RW> The parser is single-threaded. Having parsed a word, it can launch a
RW> thread per word to lookup and insert, without waiting for completion
RW> before parsing the next input word.

   Sure, but a) we were looking here for a COBOL solution, and not  
many COBOL compilers do support parallell processes (I did work with  
one, @ACOB from Unisys' OS/1100, implementing COBOL-74), and b) on a  
computer with one single CPU it is to be doubted if the saving in time  
by parallell threads is greater than the overhead of the process  
switching.

   Of course, if large amounts of time are spent by waiting for an I/ 
O-operation to complete, the saving may be substantial, but the  
parsing of a single word does not encompass I/O, only reading of the  
next line, which is probably done in larger buffers anyway.


Yours,

/--------- L.WILLMS@jpberlin.de -- Alle Rechte vorbehalten --

Wir wohnen in Gï¿½ttingen in Scheiterhaufen, die mit Tï¿½ren und Fenstern versehen sind. -G.C.Lichtenberg
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T20:59:42-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108789182.121964.74650@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<bbcd1193i5kkb17ehtco7eftvm5g81h8vp@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <1108693202.508508.319200@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com> <r2pa11thn9j6kg3ekhs62ha6r2eqe04qb9@4ax.com> <9R7h1kFPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <bbcd1193i5kkb17ehtco7eftvm5g81h8vp@4ax.com>`

```
> Nowadays, servers typically have 4-16 CPUs.

And typically run 20 - 200 processes.  Write a database server, a Web
server or an SMB server and you will _need_ to use multi-threading, or
spawn processes in other ways.

> In 2-3 years, desktops and toy computers will have more than one.

Actually you are a quarter century too late for your prediction.  The
first computer I owned had a handfull of CPUs - 8085AH2s.  One ran the
disk controller, one handled the network card, one on the serial card,
I had two application processor cards, one 8085AH2 and one with a
bit-slice to emulate an older machine.  Later I added another with a
80188 plus an 8085AH2.

My BBC Master 512 had a 6502 plus an 80186.

Desktop machines are already spending 98% of the time waiting for
keystrokes (except where Windows sucks up all the CPU for itself or for
the spyware it is running).  It is true that there will be multi-cpus,
or gpus, but they will be spending all their time rendering realistic
graphics for games or for some 3D desktop.
```

```
> Nowadays, servers typically have 4-16 CPUs.

This is not a server application.   But the reason servers have multiple CPUs is

priorities than what batch jobs get.

Some desktops have more than one already.   I can buy a Mac right now that has
more than one.   But I won't win any speed test by waiting until I can afford to
replace my computer.   And I certainly won't win any speed test by waiting 2-3
years.
```

```
>But other batch jobs are going on.

An application designer who limits his use of resources does a
disservice to his application as well as the shop. At times when all
resources are available, the job runs slower than possible for no good
reason.

Rationing scarce hardware resources is the job of the operating
system, which can adjust to the workload in real time, many times per
second. Rationing scarce intellectual development resources is the job
of programming management. When people try to 'optimize' hardware load
at design time, they aren't optimizing either, they're wasting BOTH
resources.
```

```
> At times when all resources are available, the job runs slower than
possible for no good
reason.

And at times when resources are being used the job uses more resources
than necessary.

If your jobs only gets one CPU it uses roughly a third more CPU time
than a program that only requires one CPU.

You are saving resources (time) when there is no need to do so and
wasting CPU time when resources are not available.

> When people try to 'optimize' hardware load at design time, they
aren't optimizing either,
> they're wasting BOTH resources.

And that is exactly what you are trying to do.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T14:52:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```

"Lueko Willms" <l.willms@jpberlin.de> wrote in message
news:9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de...
[snip]
>    referring to:
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> tree.

Much more likely a B+tree.

"B-tree: A tree structure that provides fast access to data
stored in files. Unlike binary trees, in which the branching
factor from a node of the tree is two, the descendants from
a node of a B-tree can be a much larger number.

"B+tree: A variation on the B-tree structure that provides
sequential access to the data as well as fast-indexed access."
Folk, Zoellick, Riccardi, "File Structures: an object-oriented
approach with C++," Addison-Wesley, 1998.

>    And it might be that the file is kept completely in memory.
>
>    So why reproduce this within my own program?

Don't reproduce it! Use only what is required. The data is all
key, by not storing the data twice, once in the index and again
in a file, and by eliminating the sequential access, performance
is improved.

If speed is a requirement, then, if all else is equal, a faster
program is a better program and the fastest program is the
best program.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T20:44:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cv5k75$709$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com>`

```

On 18-Feb-2005, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:

> If speed is a requirement, then, if all else is equal, a faster
> program is a better program and the fastest program is the
> best program.

What do we want the speed for?

The quickest way of storing data might not be the quickest way to retrieve those
same data.   Sometimes it we even want the overhead of restructuring data that
have been written quickly into a format that are more quickly retrieved.   Other
times, we can be as slow as we want in storing and/or retrieval - as long as the
other is quick.

If we are doing a one-time count of words, we don't need to store duplicates.  
But do we need to retrieve the results in alphabetical order, or by count?
```

```
That might be a reason for Mr Smith's posting, as you quoted, '... if 
all else is equal, a faster program is a better program...'.  I am 
interpreting 'all else is equal' to include the conditions under which the 
program was written and 'a faster program' to be 'the program which 
completes the required task in the least amount of consecutive wall-time 
units of measure.'

'equality'.  Mr Smith does not do that, he assumes a condition of 
equality and proceeds.

The difference here seems to be similar to the question 'What's the 
fastest way to get to Carnegie Hall?'; Mr Smith's view starts with 'Once 
you are at 42nd and 8th...' and you are saying 'Wait... you are not taking 
the transit time into account, are you arriving by train or steamboat?'
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net>

```

On 21-Feb-2005, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

> The difference here seems to be similar to the question 'What's the
> fastest way to get to Carnegie Hall?';

Practice, practice.

> Mr Smith's view starts with 'Once
> you are at 42nd and 8th...' and you are saying 'Wait... you are not taking
> the transit time into account, are you arriving by train or steamboat?'

I just want to know how we judge who is the winner.    In the movie _Cannonball
Run_, the participants of a race across the country line up at the starting
point and take their time in punching a time clock.    At the end of the movie,
they are all sprinting together as fast as they can, and somehow the person who
arrived there a second before the crowd won.   It didn't seem to fit what I
believed to be the conditions of the race.

Mr. Smith wants to know what kind of running shoes are best in that race to
Carnegie Hall.    One participant tries on two pairs of shoes as he's running on
cinder tracks and comes to a conclusion.   Another participant tries on two
pairs as he's running on concrete, and another one is doing the same on the
elevated tracks.   They come to different conclusions.

Here one person designs a program for a multi-CPU server, another designs a
program for a low memory PC, a third person designs one for a mainframe with a
highly developed keyed file system, or sort utility.   Each person can come up
with a different "right answer".

Or is the answer to "Which is faster, CoBOL or Java"?    The one running on the
Cray.  ???
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T13:59:09-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108763949.071758.128780@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<cv5k75$709$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com> <cv5k75$709$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
> What do we want the speed for?

He did say _IF_ speed is a requirement.

In this particular case the challenger will claim that his is the best
language/program/brain/penis by whatever new requirement he dreams up
to make his the 'best'.

In my view the 'best' is a program that takes the least time for one
programmer to get working correctly, is reusable, and that makes the
data available to all others so that they don't need to even write a
program.

For example using an ISAM file results in a permanent store of the
words and occurance count for anyone to access.  Bonus points for
making the program take its file names from command line parameters
and/or a configuration file or GUI so that it can be easily used with
different texts.

Even better is using a SQL database so that it is not dependent on
using the same language to access the data.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** Robert Wagner <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-19T04:06:11+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<p07d11hesal31g60b1pfjbv6ap5fvfd8l5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com> <cv5k75$709$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <1108763949.071758.128780@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 18 Feb 2005 13:59:09 -0800, "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> What do we want the speed for?
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>using the same language to access the data.

Management pays us big bucks to write code. It pays small bucks to
users of reporting tools. We have no *financial* incentive to make
ourselves obsolete. 

Mainframers have dealt with this dilemma for 40 years. Their solution
was to keep their job. This explains the failure of OO Cobol. You're
more likely to find an OO programmer from another language willing to
use OO Cobol than to find a Cobol programmer willing to use OO Cobol.
The Cobol programmer has no incentive, backed by a tradition that
survived by resisting change.

Clients new to the computing biz and seeking efficiency went with
other languages .. the ones promoting RAD. Their instincts told them
Cobol was a protectionist 'union shop'. Mainframe Coding Standards
vindicate that belief.
```

```
Standard", only to find their features are partially negated by the 
eventual J4 specifications - small wonder that there aren't 'common 
classes' available. Even given J4's target date for the 'basics' on

the feature a miss. If anything much more likely they would implement 
'Dynamic Tables' when the TR comes to fruition - and even then, probably 
likely a year or more after its official publication.

One thought though with regard to IBM - their OO is structured to pull 
in all features available from Java, with Java controlling creation and 
destruction of objects. Is it likely IBM would introduce say IBM COBOL 
collections running in tandem with the set provided by using Java.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On 18-Feb-2005, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> But do we need to retrieve the results in alphabetical order, or by count?

You could have asked, "What do we want the word count for ?". :-) How 
many times would you want to run the exercise against the King James 
Bible, the Douai version or even Jewish scrolls.

If I were to tackle it, (which I'm not going to do), here's a very broad 
outline, subject to features not 'freezing' because of size. This is 
using a cross-section of Net Express features.

1. Based on following, not clear in my mind how I would get rid of the 
text 'before' and 'after' the required text. Would have to give that 
some thought.
2. Using CBL_FILE routines read the file in ONCE as one big chunk into a 
table. (Something like 4,834,378  - so make the table 4,900,000).
3. Use UPPERCASE function against whole table.
3. Against the whole table - as Bill did with his INSPECT/REPLACING 
replace "'S" with "S " and replace the numerics and non-alpha characters 
with spaces.
4. Now invoke -

        invoke CharacterArray "withLengthValue"
                 using     ws-Length, ws-TableText
                 returning ls-String
        invoke ls-String "asArrayOfSubstrings"
               returning ls-SeparatedStrings
        invoke ls-SeparatedStrings "size" returning ws-TotalWordCount

*** My guess there's some upper limit on ls-string which might make it choke
5. ls-SeparatedStrings is an OrderedColllection containing just words - 
no spaces.
6. Turn the OrderedCollection in 5 above into a SortedCollection.
7. Now I do a callback/iterate against each element in the 
SortedCollection comparing incoming word with previous word, setting an 
individual word-count as necessary. As the word 'value' changes write to 
an Ordered Collection - the Word Value plus the frequency count.
8. Subject to #1 above, write the 'required text' to an output file.

How it would perform I have no idea. Subject to actioning the whole 
thing as a chunk, if the OO collections proved a problem - then Monsieur 
Robert could jump in and fine tune - if that was truly the problem).

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
Robert,

No you haven't. All you showed was you might have come up with a piss 
poor solution. You used a dictionary concept when perhaps there were 
other alternatives. And when I said I couldn't get your code to run, you 
took that as a criticism, rather than supplying the missing bits. 
(Purely from memory you were using a malloc and I think that may have 
been contained in another program in your test suite which I didn't have).

The only discussion I have seen on collections is the text of Adele 
Goldberg's 'Smalltalk 80' - Smalltalk is where the M/F collection 
structure is lifted from, and Hitachi COBOL too, I believe. Reading 
Adele I got the strong impression that Dictionaries as such were 
intended to be quick look-up tables - mini memory-resident ISAM files, 
if you like. (One of her examples 'Opposites' - enter the key 'Bad' and 
it gives you the opposite, Data =  'Good'). I don't think they were 
conceived as being used for large volume number-crunching. My thoughts 
versus yours - perhaps you were using the wrong solution, at the wrong 
time in the wrong place. Just because a tool doesn't fit the approach 
you want to use it for, doesn't invalidate that tool. Via the MAP Class 
Java may have put a different spin on what dictionaries are about.

Your latter comment - I'm inclined to agree. No real problem with 
collections in inter-active mode; you just don't see delays. If you are 
looking for something for number-crunching, then the 'adequate' 
collections  for GUI inter-active could be replaced by something more 
fine tuned. (And I'm guessing that is tied into the indexing algorithm).

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
On 19-Feb-2005, l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:

> JJG> You could have asked, "What do we want the word count for ?". :-)
> JJG> How many times would you want to run the exercise against the King
> JJG> James Bible, the Douai version or even Jewish scrolls.
>
>    For fun. It is just an intellectual exercise.

Part of the intellectual exercise in such a challenge is in making sure we are
all working towards the same goal.   That means defining the goal and the
measurements of success adequately.

A solution that allows users to pull up numbers quickly from an IBM mainframe
might be different from one that has a programmer write quickly on a Unisys
mainframe.    A solution that quickly runs on a Windows 98 PC might be a loser
on a 5 CPU Unix machine.    The solution that gets the number count most quickly
to a user most likely is the one that takes the least amount of code and might
even be interpreted.    Is there a different word count for the word "god" and
the name "God"?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T13:47:36-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108763256.287861.216210@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com>`

```
> If speed is a requirement, then, if all else is equal, a
> faster program is a better program and the fastest
> program is the best program.

I started playing with this problem and decided that it would be useful
to separate out the timing for the various parts of the problems.  To
save time I wrote a simple parser in Python to extract the words to a
text file so that I could try Cobol sort and isam without bothering
with doing this in Cobol yet.

Just as a test I added the handful of lines to store away these words
in a 'dictionary'.  The result was 6.3 seconds (AMD 2MHz) to discover
the 12700 unique words and count their occurances.

Python is an interpreted (JIT compiled) scripting language.  It took me
about 20 minutes to code the problem and get it working exactly as I
wanted it in about 40 lines.  It counts each occurance of the unique
words and outputs a file of all words.

Taking out the dictionary and word file gives a run-time of 4.2 sec
which is just reading the text and parsing the words, converting to
lower and stripping out the non-letters.

This means that using the dictionary and writing the words to a file
takes 2 seconds.

We need to do _much_ better than this with Cobol given that it can be
fully compiled.

A simple Fujitsu Cobol program takes 3 seconds to read the line
sequential file of the 789,759 words (one per line) that was written by
Python.  Adding writing to an ISAM makes it 12.  Thus the ISAM file
consumes 9 seconds when it is only used to WRITE and nothing is done
when a '22' error occurs, ie no counting occurances.

Interestingly using READ and WRITE or REWRITE takes just over 11
seconds and caters for counting occurances.

Using SORT USING words OUTPUT PROCEDURE .. gives 8.5 seconds, thus the
SORT itself is around 5.5 sec.

As this was reading the same Python word file it may be assumed that
doing the text file parsing into words may make the program longer,
though it is only about 0.5 sec to read the 114,000 lines of the text.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T16:34:41-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108773281.940245.115580@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1108763256.287861.216210@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com> <1108763256.287861.216210@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Update:

I decided to optimise the Python script by putting the translate of the
characters onto the file line instead of on each word indiviually.
This took the time down to 4.9sec from 6.3.

Then I removed the writing of the word file which had been done for
checking as well as being for input to Cobol:

114100  lines
789781  words
12691  unique
3.7043299675  seconds

Remove the counting of the occurances of each unique word:

114100  lines
789781  words
12691  unique
3.15204501152  seconds

Now I changed it to read the whole file into memory and process the
translate that replaces everthing except letters to spaces on the whole
text in one expression (instead of one per line):

789953  words
12727  unique
2.48051190376  seconds

This didn't drop the first 44 lines or the last few so there are more
words.

Now a couple more optimisations and:

789953  words
12727  unique
0.916288852692  seconds

Here's the code of this version:

#!/bin/env python
import string
import time
letter_table = "                                " \
             + "                                " \
             + " abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz     " \
             + " abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz     " \
             + "                                " \
             + "                                " \
             + "                                " \
             + "                                "
start_time = time.time()
lines = open("lines", "r")
buf = lines.read()
worddic = {}
xbuf = string.translate(buf, letter_table,"'")
wordlist = string.split(xbuf)
for word in wordlist:
    worddic[word] = 1
lines.close()
print len(wordlist), " words"
print len(worddic), " unique"
print time.time() - start_time, " seconds"
```

```
> Brilliant! I'm impressed.

Well, _I_ was impressed too.  Mainly that I could get it run in under a
second.

It's now up to 1.2 sec because I added back some code to count
occurrances:

for word in wordlist:       # iterates over the 800,000 words
    try:
         worddic[word] += 1   # add 1 to the entry in the dictionary
    except:                          # if it fails
         worddic[word] = 1      # create a new entry

That's it, that builds a structure accessed by key.  No fiddling memory
addresses.  I neither know nor care how it works, whether it is a
b-tree or a linked list, it just works.

To list in sorted order:

wordlist = worddic.keys()       # get a list of all keys
wordlist.sort()                       # sort to alpha sequence
for word in wordlist:               # iterate
    print word, wordlist[word]
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1108763256.287861.216210@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com...
> > If speed is a requirement, then, if all else is equal, a
> > faster program is a better program and the fastest
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> though it is only about 0.5 sec to read the 114,000 lines of the text.

I wrote a subprogram to extract the words. The subprogram reads
the input as a byte stream file and is tuned for the best speed.
When I comment out the RELEASE statement in the sort version,
it runs in 0.39s. With the RELEASE statement, it runs in 21.86s.
The ISAM version runs in 27.08s. (2.4GHz AMD Athlon XP)

Apparently, the overhead for my 16-bit Micro Focus 3.2 SORT
and ISAM is much greater than you experienced. Thus, it might
be possible that I could use an in-memory solution to improve
the performance significantly.

My first attempt was an in-memory solution using hashing. It
worked as expected, in intermediate code; but, when compiled,
failed miserably with subscript range errors when it approached
the end of a 64K segment. I moved the tables to relative files and
it worked; but, when I ran it to get the hash index calculation times,
found it took 76s just to calculate the hash indexes. There was, as
I recall, an additional 11s for file processing.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-20T13:11:06-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<111hkq8lmn0rva8@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <111fefpjskr1664@corp.supernews.com> <111g0vt6l5g7e12@corp.supernews.com> <9RFya5KeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```

"Lueko Willms" <l.willms@jpberlin.de> wrote in message
news:9RFya5KeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de...
> .    On  19.02.05
>   wrote  ricksmith@mfi.net (Rick Smith)
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> mind what this means, and how it is done?

The virtual heap file, as I use it, is similar to a relative file; except
that byte relative positions are used instead of relative record
numbers. Or, if you prefer, byte relative positions instead of
subscripts to a table.

The calculation of the hash index was done by converting the text
to a set of numbers ( char, xchar ) <- (a, 1), (b, 2), ..., (z, 26).
The numbers where then combined mathematically. Essentially,

    move 0 to hash-index
    perform varying x from 1 by 1 until char (x) = space
        compute hash-index = function mod (
            (hash-index * 17 + ( xchar (x) )) 8192)
    end-perform
    add 1 to hash-index

The resulting hash-index is used as a subscript to a "scatter table",
which holds the starting positions for up to 8192 linked lists, one
for each index. I have 6455 (78.8%) linked lists with a maximum
of 7 entries.

The increased speed (over binary tree) is the result of reducing
the number of key comparisons to, in this case, no more than 7.

>    I can't remember that you have presented your solutions for others
> to see.

I have not. While the language is COBOL, the methods are C.
The above code is actually closer to:

    hash_index = 0;
    for (x = 0; char(x) != ' '; x++)
        hash_index = (hash_index * 17 + (char(x) & 0x1F)) & 0x1FFF;

Because this relies on the ASCII character set, showing the
COBOL code might cause, at least temporary, consternation for
those more familiar with EBCDIC and might do the same for
those whose "love" of COBOL cannot easily tolerate the language
extensions I used or how I used them.
```

```
there is something very interesting in this two sources: the benchmark 
timing is not done at the same place ! in the C++ sources, it stops 
before the output of the result, in Fortran, it stops after...

well, it should also be really interresting to know the speed of the 
drive used to make the benchmark: I/O routines for disk access are 
especially slow (compared to memory I/O) and old 5400 RPM disk on a 
33MHz IDE won't perform the same as a 10000RPM on Ultra Wide SCSI2 disk.

eventually, we may also consider the screen output on a particular 
system with a particular graphic card, the size of the processor 
cache... so this challenge seems more like a hardware benchmark than 
of a real programming challenge !

processor, we may also expect it to have all the latest and fastest 
technologies in PC computing. how then can you compare yourself to the
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol,comp.lang.pl1

- **In-Reply-To:** `<111njuk74313u0e@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <111fefpjskr1664@corp.supernews.com> <111g0vt6l5g7e12@corp.supernews.com> <9RFya5KeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111hkq8lmn0rva8@corp.supernews.com> <111hvadtpeu3e4c@corp.supernews.com> <4219b56b$0$39323$ec3e2dad@news.usenetmonster.com> <111k7fimf7vag86@corp.supernews.com> <111njuk74313u0e@corp.supernews.com>`

```
Rick Smith wrote:
[snip]
>     0.66 Sec 2.4GHz AMD
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Is that no longer correct for that source code?

try these softwares on a different computer...

just for the record (and even if it does not concern CoBOL or PL/I) i 
tried it in Ada, using the 26-way tree technique suggested by Richard 
on comp.lang.cobol. results are:

0.54 sec. Athlon XP2000 (slightly less than 2 GHz)
0.78 sec. Pentium4 3.0 GHz

why such a difference ? my 3.0GHz computer holds my old 5400RPM IDE 
hard drive, my Athlon XP2000 uses a (more) modern 7200RPM ATA 100 hard 
drive... unless we all test on the same drive results are screwed up.

a comment about the different techniques tried by each candidate 
implementation: each techniques being a map, an array, a database, a 
n-way tree or anything else is valid. each candidate in each language 
tried to use what was the more efficient for their language.
C++ having a standard containers, use of a map was evident. CoBOL, 
made for business and huge data processing used database files. 
Fortran being an old language lacking more modern techniques used a 
straightforward procedural approach. Ada allowing to declare high 
level structures, the use of a 26-way tree was simpler. I think a Perl 
programmer would have used a regular expression, and a Haskell 
programmer a function which would retrieve the count for a specific 
word at the last moment...

each language has its weaknesses as well as its own strengths, each 
candidate was implemented with this in mind. rather than seeing a 
difference in the technique used as a "that is not what was asked" or 
a "this is one of your language's can't", it should be seen as the 
expression of the great diversity which exists in the computing world 
and shows that we all have something to learn from other communities.

i hope everyone will come to the same conclusion...
```

```
Note input/output time is about 10% of my total runtime of 0.88 sec
```

```
ok, i made some optimizations on my Ada solutions:
- i use another method to read the file
- i read the file as a whole in an array then make the processing on the 
array

the new version is less portable, since it does not read characters but 
rather bytes, so it won't work with non-ascii input, but it is the same 
for many other candidates i think.

timing result:
0.16 seconds on my Athlon XP2000 (pseudo 2GHz) divided into 0.019s 
reading the file, and 0.14s processing data (i do not count the output).

the updated version can be found here:
http://www.stochastique.net/~rien/dev/ada/bible/bible_new.adb
```

```

"David Frank" <dave_frank@hotmail.com> wrote in message
news:421db712$0$95898$ec3e2dad@news.usenetmonster.com...
>
> "Adrien Plisson" <aplisson-news@stochastique.net> wrote in message
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> > drive, my Athlon XP2000 uses a (more) modern 7200RPM ATA 100 hard
drive...
> > unless we all test on the same drive results are screwed up.
> >
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 1. allocate space/input the file into a text array, this would be TIME1

That's a 32-bit solution. It wouldn't work for 16-bits.
I provide 24K for a buffer, which is filled about 200 times.
I must process the text into words without loading the entire
file at the start.

> 2. process the text into words counts, this would be TIME2
> 3. output the sorted results to a file, this would be TIME3

I found, that processing the text into words, without saving
the words or counting them, takes about 0.11s.

The additional time to save and count the words is about 0.28s.

Sorting and output is about 0.05s.

My latest version runs in 0.44s.

Add 0.22s to account for the OS caching the entire file
between runs, then single execution time is about 0.66s.

My point is that there are many times that could be measured;
but most have no meaning, except to the developer profiling
the program to see where improvements are needed.
```

```
and its a allocated size, what more does he want?

>   As to map, I find this in the source program, which needs some
> explanation from a Fortran programmer:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It allows the text to be formed into words as array of chars, and then in 
the quicksort the words are accessed/sorted using Fortran's string syntax.

Normally this is done to ordinary declarations using  EQUIVALENCE statement 
(and there are examples of that going on as well).  But equivalence  is not 
allowed by Fortran for variables within the type declaration but the union 
map syntax effectively is an equivalence thats allowed with type 
declarations.
```

```
RW> Program logic was not specific to the Christian Bible. Any large text

   Well, not really. The specifications were very specific and do not

   The idea to use the number of words in a text as a measure for the  
size of that text is very specific to then english language which, as  
an isolating language, does not have many long words. In many other  
languages, that obsession makes no sense.
```

```
Sometime last year, I read a copy of, I believe, the FDIS for
the latest Fortran. As I understood it, the Fortran standard
includes, or will include, the C standard. If I understood the
relationship correctly, then any conforming C program is a
conforming Fortran program; but not the reverse; thus it would
not matter whether the program was claimed to be C or Fortran
when comparing it to C++.
```

```
On Sun, 27 Feb 2005 16:19:30 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:
<snip>
> Sometime last year, I read a copy of, I believe, the FDIS for
> the latest Fortran. As I understood it, the Fortran standard
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Nowhere near. Among the several features added in "F2003", which was
actually approved in 2004, is "C interoperabilty" which provides a
series of types (or more precisely what Fortran calls KINDs) and some
declaration attributes which will allow Fortran code to access C
variables and functions (procedures) and/or provide variables and
procedures accessible from C on the same platform. This does involve
some _references_ to the C standard to identify unambiguously the
things with which compatibility is provided.

- David.Thompson1 at worldnet.att.net
```

```
> The 3 claims?
> 1. more readable solutions  (definitely)

What is 'more readable' is entirely dependent on 'what one is used to'.
 A Japanese may find something 'more readable' while you find it
resembles hen scratchings.

If you haven't used C++ extensively you won't find it particularly
readable.  If a C++ programmer hasn't worked with Fortran they won't
find it readable.

> 2. briefer     (debatable)

Pointless.  The briefest solution is Python which does the core of the
work in 6 lines of code.

> 3. faster      ( much faster for this word-processing
> benchmark,

Generally Fortran can be faster than C or C++ because it need not do
promotion and because the constructs lend themselves to better
optimisation.

This is usually irrelevant, but wasn't in the 1970s.
```

```
I should say the easiest to read may well be Ruby (I haven't been 
following this thread in detail, so there may be some differences from 
the latest bunch of specs).

start = Time.new
words = {}; words.default = 0
wordcount = 0
ARGV.each do |filename|
  File.open(filename) do |input|
   input.each do |line|
    line.downcase.gsub(/'/, '').gsub(/[^a-z]+/, ' ').split.each do |word|
     wordcount += 1; words[word] += 1
    end
   end
  end
end
words.keys.sort.each do |word|
  printf "%6d: %s\n", words[word], word
end
print "total wordcount: #{wordcount}\n"
print "unique wordcount: #{words.length}\n"
print "'god': #{words['god']}\n"
print "time: #{Time.new-start}s\n"

It's not as fast as Perl on my system, though.

---

corrupts."
```

```
>>  http://home.cfl.rr.com/davegemini/wc_file.c

   Well, that Fortran does not look like the Fortran which I learned a  
quarter of a century ago.


>> AND as I told LR in the beginning,  his C++ challenge using MAP syntax
>> likely has lots of overhead, and now my guess is confirmed as his
>> version  compiles/runs 10x slower on my computer.

   The main difference between the languages is, how well it eases the  
task of a programmer to specify the transformations his or her program  
is to perform. The time needed to develop the program, or rather  
system of programs, the safety of the programs is a decisive factor.

   After that, individual style applies, and the differences in  
running time result to a very large part from choices made by the  
programmer, not the possibilities of the language.

SA> Fine. The running times are getting way too low to be meaningful. Use
SA> a bigger text corpus. Using these files from Project Gutenberg
SA> concatenated into a single file without any editing
SA>
SA> http://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/etext92/bible13.zip
SA> http://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/etext94/shaks12.zip
SA> http://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/3/2/0/3200/3200.zip
SA> http://www.gutenberg.org/dirs/etext02/mthes10.zip

   How large is that combined file?

SA> total words  : 8'398'516
SA> unique words :   102'515


SA> The result for Perl is:
SA> time         : 24.657499 s.
SA>
SA>
SA> And for Python:
SA> time         : 32.980047 s.

   As one can see from the above quoted link to this Fortran program,  
this reads in the whole text file in one big chunk. The C program  
seems to do the same, although I am not so familiar with the symbolism  
of C to really understand it. This speeds up the processing of the  
file.

   But what if the textfile to search has, say, 1 Gigabyte of bytes?  
Or any number of bytes larger than the available main memory? The  
program starts swapping, losing the advantage of reading the file as  
one chunk.

   Also, the program is tuned to this one task as specified by the  
author of that program, but will fail with other character sets (even  
a US-American "Resumï¿½" would not appear as a word) and languages or  
any word longer than 30 characters. In agglutinating languages like  
German or Welsh, a word can have easily more than 100 characters.
```

```
> re:    100 char words,  you poor souls...

Yes, well maybe not *easily* more than 100, but you can have plenty 
long words in Swedish, as well as in German and many other languages. 
Since Welsh was mentioned, the program should cater for 
Llanfairpwllgwyngyllgogerychwyrndrobwllllantysiliogogogoch 
(even though that is actually several words).

Or this, an actual Swedish word of 54 characters:
realisationsvinstbeskattningstabellradsummerubrikernas.

> I have tried my hand at adding extended ascii chars to my program,
>   have a look at its processing of 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> source at   http://home.cfl.rr.com/davegemini/wc_file.f90

Here is a version of the Python program that uses the current system 
locale for word recognition, lower casing and sort. Note that this 
program can easily be extended to support full Unicode text:

import locale, re, string, sys, time

if len(sys.argv) != 3: sys.exit("Usage: frank.py infile countfile")

locale.setlocale(locale.LC_ALL, '')

trim_nws = re.compile("[^"+string.lowercase+"]+")
words = {}

start = time.clock()
for line in open(sys.argv[1]):
    for word in re.sub(trim_nws, " ", line.lower().replace("'", "")).split():
        try: words[word] += 1
        except KeyError: words[word] = 1
time1 = time.clock()

sorted_keys = words.keys(); sorted_keys.sort()
time2 = time.clock()

outfile = open(sys.argv[2], "w+")
for word in sorted_keys: 
    print >> outfile, "%6d %s" % (words[word], word)
time3 = time.clock()

print "total words  : %d" % sum(words.values())
print "unique words : %d" % len(words)
print "times        : %f, %f, %f s." % (time1 - start, time2 - time1, time3 - time2)
print "total time   : %f s." % (time3 - start)
print "1.5 GHz P4 Mobile"
```

```
David Frank wrote:
> I'm pretty sure its because of the  '  parsing..
> 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> discard them all.

that's indeed really strange parsing ' causes that much headaches !

if you are reading the file character by character, then you can just 
ignore those pesky little quotes:
- if it is surrouded by letters, then you will get the entire word with 
all letters concatenated and without the quote, the word will be counted 
as specified in the problem
- if it is not, that is there are characters other than letters either 
before or after the word, then this other character will be treated as a 
separator, thus ignoring the quote will not influence the results

so why bother doing a special processing on the quotes when you can just 
do nothing ?

> we could change the rules and just leave them alone.

just don't change the rules, they already implicitly specify to leave 
them alone...
```

```
running time. The dictionary has to be exported into an array to read  
it sequentially, and the array has been sorted by a Quicksort which I  
ported to VBS. This Quicksort added only a few fractions of a second  
to the running time.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-18T21:10:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108789834.989840.282640@l41g2000cwc.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ja5d119pcdthuqq3fh8b2id1kq06rghf4p@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com> <ja5d119pcdthuqq3fh8b2id1kq06rghf4p@4ax.com>`

```
> What's the optimal number? There must be one.

Why must there be _one_ ?

B-Trees are normally implemented on disk and the 'optimal number' for
any given B-tree is most likely the number that will fit in disk's unit
of transfer. This depends on the OS, the disk, the size of the key, and
possibly other factors.  For high levels of activity the nodes will be
lightly packed.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2005-02-19T02:52:03-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<111ds4m2mk4kd15@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <arga119ci214i0sjrrks90tqj6h1occkec@4ax.com> <111alniicrtje24@corp.supernews.com> <9R7h2QL9flB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <111chus37ogfm58@corp.supernews.com> <ja5d119pcdthuqq3fh8b2id1kq06rghf4p@4ax.com>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <spamblocker-robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:ja5d119pcdthuqq3fh8b2id1kq06rghf4p@4ax.com...
> On Fri, 18 Feb 2005 14:52:18 -0500, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
[snip]
> >"B-tree: A tree structure that provides fast access to data
> >stored in files. Unlike binary trees, in which the branching
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> What's the optimal number? There must be one.

Richard has mentioned some factors when using disk. When
using memory, one factor could be whether space for the nodes
is pre-allocated. Calling 'malloc', for example, has overhead
and, in my experience, returns a block that is a multiple of some
fixed size, such as a multiple of 128 bytes. Where the key is
small, a B-tree node that fits 128 bytes might be better than
a binary tree, since this would reduce the overhead for memory
allocation; but I don't have any evidence to support this and I
have been wrong before.

> This question came up in
> connection with Shell Sort, where it was never answered. Based on
> simple calculus and experimental trials, I postulated it was e+1 =
> 3.71. Further investigation cause me to lose confidence in that
> number. I now think it might be 2 after all.

Sedgewick, "Algorithms in C," states that the increment 2 ** k
(1, 2, 4, 8, ...) has poor performance under some circumstances.
Knuth, "The Art of Computer Programming, Volume 3: Sorting
and Searching," suggests that increment 3 ** k + 1 (1, 4, 13,
40, ...), while not always fastest, has less loss of performance
when it is not optimal.
```

```
As I recall, the mention of a sorted list occurred later and not by
the originator or his adjutant; thus it was never part of the challenge.

The original post suggested arrarys with 65536 elements, which
```

```
Depending upon the actual "real world" <G> environment in which the information
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com

```
(I was away for the weekend)  If I never posted it, I am running on a 1.3 GHz 
Pentium 4; Windows XP (Using Fujitsu V7)

The reason that I coded an "ANSI conforming" solution was so that it COULD (just 
change the Select/Assign) be run on ANY (PC, Workstation, Mainframe) compute 
with an ANSI '85 (and '89) COBOL compiler.

This is true of both WORDCNT1 and WORDCNT2 (Although I think I left the 
debugging lines in WORDCNT1 - so they might need to be removed if your compiler 
doesn't support that optional module).
```

```
LX-i retorted:

<<Only to the paranoid...>>

Naah.

I too wonder about the association of the prominence of the promotion of the
King James Bible, without any reference to any other translation or any
questions about the 15th-to-17th-century scholarship that underlies it, with
the concept of "patriotism".   I have great respect for the King James
particularly as a scholarly translation of the Greek Text that underlies its
New Testament, but I would have to admit some alarm at the fact that the
Douay-Rheims that's relatively contemporary with it is nowhere mentioned on
this "patriot" website.

The only version of the Bible downloadable from this "patriotic" website is
the King James.

Are there any other versions of the Bible that can legitimately be accepted
as "patriotic"?  What about other writings people hold sacred, like the
Koran and the Book of Mormon?  What of those that reject the New Testament
outright?  What of the Apocrypha that Protestants reject and Catholics
accept?  Are those who accept such books as "inspired" alongside, or as
alternative to, the King James Bible somehow intrinsically less patriotic?
King James Bibles published for the first couple of centuries included the
Apocrypha; only much later were they deleted.  Should a "patriotic" word
count be based on a King James Bible that *includes* the Apocrypha, or must
one restrict oneself to one that does not?

I have to agree that the *implication* that the King James Bible (of any
vintage!) is THE patriotic Bible (implicit in its being so prominently
featured in a website that also prominently features the American flag)
differs only in degree from the attitude I read of in recent news, that
Group A is comprised of the TRUE followers of Mohammed and Group B is a
bunch of infidels that fundamentally deserve to be exterminated like
cockroaches.  It doesn't much matter to me which particular group is Group A
and Group B; my problem is with the fact that there is so much animosity and
contempt (veiled or otherwise) comingling politics with religion.

I have acquaintances that make a big point of Demonstrating their Patriotism
with a combination of American flags flying from their car's side windows
with really-insulting anti-Islam stickers on their bumpers.

I have run into the attitude that neither a Jew nor a Mormon can have
anything positive to offer society in business, in society or in government
precisely because they aren't True Believers.  Evidence of such attitudes
can be overt -- and I have encountered it explicitly and overtly in the
workplace -- but it can also be subtle, and it's subtle associations like
"King James Bible = Patriotic" that strike me, apparently along with Peter
Lacey, as "mildly alarming".

I had a co-worker inform me one time that his religion obligated him to haul
me out in front of the workplace and, along with all the other True
Believers to stone me to death on the front steps of our workplace; it was a
direct violation of the God-given Bill of Rights for the state to prevent
him from doing so.

I had a different co-worker inform me that his religion taught him that a
Jew cannot have any integrity in any context or circumstance unless and
until he or she accepts Christianity, and that therefore his religion
obligated him to ensure -- "by hook or by crook" -- to ensure that
management was not deluded into thinking that they did.

Perhaps you have not personally encountered such circumstances.  But I have,
and I have to agree with Peter's characterization of the association of
"King James Version" with "patriot" as "mildly alarming".

I mistrust individuals as well as groups that identify themselves or their
views with self-promoting adjectives like "patriotic", "humble", "expert",
"proper Christian" and so forth.

    -Chuck Stevens.
```

```
Chuck Stevens wrote:
> LX-i retorted:
>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> "patriot" website.

The particular translation is actually irrelevant to the devout. It really 
doesn't matter what the Bible says; what is important is what it means. 
Often God didn't mean what He said and sometimes didn't say what He meant. 
So, like the Constitution, it comes down to interpretation.

In the Jewish tradition, the mechanism and the authority for interpretation 
was passed down with the text.

In the Catholic tradition, the authority for interpretation lies with the 
Church as an institution.

In the non-Catholic tradition, authority for interpretation lies with the 
individual invested with and assisted by the Holy Spirit. In this latter 
case the Bible verse "See Spot Run," could be God's absolute word on sex 
with plants.
```

#### ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2005-02-14T03:46:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4aVPd.2085380$f47.365399@news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com>`

```
I have created a COBOL program that does what this is asking for.  However, 
before looking at what I have done:

1) See separate note on the fact that my results match the "unique" word count - 
but not the "total" word counts - expected. (Don't ask me why).

2) My sample includes TONS of "debugging lines" that I kept in to show (COBOL 
readers) how I worked my way to this solution (sort of <G>)

3) Rather than using ANY "sort" statement, I have used a (temporary) INDEXED 
file - using "FS=02" to count duplicate words.  I can NOT believe this is the 
most "efficient" run-time solution, but it does show one "facility" of COBOL.

4) I compiled with Fujitsu - but used the @OPTIONS statement to check for 
non-Standard code.  Fujitsu is NOTORIOUSLY week and extension flagging, so I may 
have missed some extensions to the '85 Standard - but the only one I know about 
is using LINE SEQUENTIAL for ht input file.  (I even used A-/B-margin rules <G>)

  ***

I fully expect that are LOTS of other ways to do this in COBOL - certainly 
PERFORM loops may well out-perform UNSTRING and INSPECT statements (that I use 
*heavily*)

For what I have done, check out:

   http://home.comcast.net/~wmklein/DOX/WORDCNT.txt
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-15T09:53:45-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1108490025.639649.93120@g14g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-C004E9.00405915022005@knology.usenetserver.com> <9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
> Also, I am not sure what you mean by "terminus" -- to me this is
> the train station, or a term in its original latin form :-))

A 'terminal' or 'terminus' is not just any train or bus station, it is
the one at the end of the line or route. It is where the route
terminates.  In the case of a 'text-tree' it is where the branch that
represents one word terminates.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-02-16T23:08:04-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-20564A.23080316022005@knology.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-C004E9.00405915022005@knology.usenetserver.com> <9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
In article <9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>,
 l.willms@jpberlin.de (Lueko Willms) wrote:

> .    On  15.02.05
>   wrote  joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com (Joe Zitzelberger)
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

I use the chars of the word as an index into a tree.  The terminus would 
be the node at the last character, there I would keep a counter for the 
number of instances.

You would create a node-item that looks something like:

   01 A-Node.
      05 occurs 27.
         10 an-ptr   usage pointer.
         10 an-cnt   pic s9(8) binary.
   

Then you would have a program like so:

   program-id. add2tree is recursive.
  * 
  * This program takes a word of length word-ll
  * and uses it as an index into the tree based
  * in anode.
  *
  * If a path exists, the count at the last indexed
  * node is incremented.
  * 
  * If the path does not exist, the tree nodes are 
  * allocated until all of the indexes are gone.
  *
  * E.G A tree with 'apple' and 'apply' and 'ape' 
  * would look thus:
  *
  * an['a']->an['p']--->an['p']->an['l']--->an['e'] = 1
  *                  |                   |
  *                  |->an['e'] = 1      |->an['y'] = 1
  *
  * As this is an example program, it is assumed that 
  * word is a-z or apos only.  No edits are done.
  *
   data division.
   working storage section.
   01 II          pic s9(8) binary.
   01 ch2ix       pix x(27) 
      value "ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ'".

   linkage section.
      copy anode.
   01 word-ll     pic s9(4) binary.
   01 word-txt    pic x(25).

   procedure division using anode
                            word-ll
                            word-txt.

      perform char-to-index
      if word-ll = 1
        perform increment-node
      else
        perform locate-node
        subtract 1 from word-ll
        call 'add2tree' using
           a-node
           word-ll
           word-txt (2:)
        end-call
      end-if

      goback.

  *
  * returns index of char in II
  *
   char-to-index.
      perform varying II from 1 by 1
        until (II > length of ch2ix)
           or (ch2ix (II) = word-txt (1:1))
        continue
      end-perform
      .      

  *
  * positions current node based on word(1), alloc if not found
  *
   locate-node.
      if an-ptr (II) = null
         ... do platform specific allocate of a 216-bytes here...
      end-if

      set address of A-Node
        to address of an-ptr (II)
      .      

  *
  * adds one to the terminal node in the tree for this word
  *
   increment-node.            
      add 1 to an-ctr (II)
      .      


Yes, yes, I know I could have removed the tail recursion if I really 
wanted to.  But it is cleaner this way, and with most words being in the 
sub 10-char range, it really doesn't make much difference.

Once all the words are passed to the program, just read through in 
pre-order and write out the count that represents the number of word 
instances.
```

```
JZ>          10 an-cnt   pic s9(8) binary.

   Ah, you see. It needs a recursive data structure and a recursive  
algorithm.

   But which COBOL compiler does support recursive calls of a program  
to itself?

   Is that regulated in some standard?

   BTW, I have written my version in COBOL, and runs only a little bit  
more than a minute (1:06,07) on a 350-MHz Windows NT 4.0 server with  
256 MByte RAM. The lookup for unique words is done by writing each and  
every word to an ISAM file which has as only field the word, which is  
the primary key. When the file status indicates "Duplicate key", I  
increment the count of unique words (the specification of that  
challenge does not require to count the frequency of the individual  
unique words). I rely completely on the Fujitsu run-time system to  
build an efficient tree for the index and to manage the file buffers.

   But :-(( the parser which I have presented in another message does  
not work correctly. For a lot of words it cuts one or more characters  
at the beginning of the word, and the counts are:

   my result  challenge
    16'747      12'691     unique words
   535'208     789'781     total words


   So it still needs some work.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-02-17T08:48:44-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cv2htc$2ock$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-20564A.23080316022005@knology.usenetserver.com> <9R3Z1DEeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```

"Lueko Willms" <l.willms@jpberlin.de> wrote in message
news:9R3Z1DEeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de...

>    Ah, you see. It needs a recursive data structure and a recursive
> algorithm.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>    Is that regulated in some standard?

ISO/IEC 1989:2002 allows recursive programs, methods and functions.  See the
description of and rules for the RECURSIVE clause in 11.9, PROGRAM-ID
paragraph on Pages 186-187.

Recursive calls were explicitly disallowed by ANSI X3.23-1985 / ISO
1989-1985 (page X-30, 5.2, The CALL statement, general rule 15) as well as
ANSI X3.23-1974.  .

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cross-language challenge (of POSSIBLE interest)

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-02-17T09:40:54-08:00

- **Message-ID:** `<1108662054.545867.263710@c13g2000cwb.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9R3Z1DEeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<G1PPd.2059185$f47.360893@news.easynews.com> <9QwEwCfeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-20564A.23080316022005@knology.usenetserver.com> <9R3Z1DEeflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
> Ah, you see. It needs a recursive data structure and a recursive
algorithm.

There is no requirement for the compiler to support recursion, it can
be done equally well with iteration.

In fact all that recursion does is push a new stack frame.  It is easy
enough to maintain your own 'stack' using an array and 'recurse' in a
perform loop.
```
